border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_English_history" title="Timeline of English history">Timeline</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Periods_in_English_history" title="Template:Periods in English history"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Periods_in_English_history" title="Template talk:Periods in English history"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Periods_in_English_history&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>social history of the United Kingdom from 1945</b> began with the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World War</a>. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> was one of the victors, but victory was costly in social and economic terms. Thus, the late-1940s was a time of austerity and economic restraint, which gave way to prosperity in the 1950s. The <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Party (United Kingdom)">Labour Party</a> led by wartime <a href="/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Deputy Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Deputy Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a>, won the <a href="/wiki/1945_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1945 UK general election">1945 postwar general election</a> in a landslide, against all odds, and formed a majority government for the first time ever. Labour governed until <a href="/wiki/1951_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1951 UK general election">1951</a>, and granted <a href="/wiki/Indian_Independence_Act_1947" title="Indian Independence Act 1947">independence to India</a> in 1947. Most of the other major overseas colonies became independent in the late-1950s and early-1960s. The UK collaborated closely with the United States during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> after 1947, and in 1949; helped to form <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> as a military alliance against the spread of Soviet Communism. After a long debate and initial scepticism, the United Kingdom joined the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> along with <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> on 1 January 1973, but voted to leave the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> in a <a href="/wiki/UK_EU_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="UK EU referendum">nationwide referendum held on 23 June 2016</a>. Immigration from South Asia, the West Indies, and Eastern Europe laid the foundations for the modern-day multicultural society in today\'s Britain, while traditional Anglican and other denominations of Christianity declined sharply.\n</p><p>Prosperity returned in the 1950s, reaching the middle-class and, to a large extent, the working-class across Britain. <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> remained a world centre of finance and culture, but the nation was no longer a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a>. In foreign policy, the UK promoted the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth</a> (in the economic sphere) and the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Alliance" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic Alliance">Atlantic Alliance</a> (in the military sphere). In domestic policy, a <a href="/wiki/Post-war_consensus" title="Post-war consensus">post-war consensus</a> saw the leadership of the Labour and Conservative parties largely agreed on <a href="/wiki/Keynesian" class="mw-redirect" title="Keynesian">Keynesian</a> policies, with support for <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unions</a>, regulation of business, and nationalisation of many older industries. The discovery of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> eased some financial pressures, but the 1970s saw slow economic growth, rising unemployment, and escalating labour strife. Deindustrialisation or the loss of heavy industry, especially coal mining, shipbuilding and manufacturing, grew worse after 1970 as the British economy shifted to services. London and the <a href="/wiki/South_East_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="South East of England">South East</a> maintained prosperity, as London became the leading financial centre in Europe and played a major role in world affairs.\n</p><p>Higher education expanded rapidly and attracted an international clientele, while debates raged on the elitist effect of grammar schools. The status of women slowly improved. A youth culture emerged from the 1960s with such iconic international celebrities as: <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">The Rolling Stones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Princess_Diana" class="mw-redirect" title="Princess Diana">Princess Diana</a>. Conservative Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> (1979-1990) rejected the post-war consensus in the 1980s, privatised most state-owned industries, and worked to weaken the power and influence of the trade unions. The "<a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a>" premiership of <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> (1997\xe2\x80\x932007) accepted most of Thatcher\'s economic policies. Devolution became a major topic, as Scotland and Wales gained more local control following referenda held in 1997. In 2014, a <a href="/wiki/2014_Scottish_independence_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="2014 Scottish independence referendum">referendum on Scottish independence</a> was held, and Scotland voted 55% to 45% to remain part of the UK.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 href="#Thatcher&#39;s_Britain"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Thatcher\'s Britain</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Thatcherism"><span 1997</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-41"><a href="#Tony_Blair_and_New_Labour"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Tony Blair and New Labour</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-42"><a href="#Conservatives_return"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Conservatives return</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-43"><a href="#Economic_growth_and_crises_since_1995"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Economic growth and crises since 1995</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-44"><a href="#Housing_2"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Housing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-45"><a href="#Brexit"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Brexit</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-46"><a href="#Historiography"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-47"><a href="#Post-war_consensus"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Post-war consensus</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-48"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-49"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-50"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-51"><a href="#Popular_social_history"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Popular social history</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-52"><a href="#Statistics"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Statistics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-53"><a href="#Historiography_2"><span class="tocnumber">9.3</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_era">Post-war era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Post-war era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Age_of_Austerity">Age of Austerity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Age of Austerity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1945" title="United Kingdom general election, 1945">United Kingdom general election, 1945</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Attlee_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/Attlee_cropped.jpg" width="180" height="235" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="235" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Attlee_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> was Prime Minister from 1945 to 1951.</div></div></div>\n<p>In May 1945, the governing coalition dissolved, triggering the long-overdue 1945 general election.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> Labour won just under 50% of the vote and a majority of 145 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The new Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Clement_Attlee" title="Clement Attlee">Clement Attlee</a> proclaimed, "This is the first time in the history of the country that a labour movement with a socialist policy has received the approval of the electorate."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKynaston201075_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKynaston201075-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the war, surveys showed public opinion moving to the left and in favour of wide social reform.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The public associated the Conservative Party with the poverty and mass unemployment of the inter-war years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan1985Chapter_1_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan1985Chapter_1-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Henry_Pelling" title="Henry Pelling">Henry Pelling</a>, noting that polls showed a steady Labour lead after 1942, points to the usual swing against the party in power; the Conservative loss of initiative; wide fears of a return to the high unemployment of the 1930s; the theme that socialist planning would be more efficient in operating the economy; and the mistaken belief that Churchill would continue as prime minister regardless of the result.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The sense that all Britons had joined in a "People\'s War" and all deserved a reward animated voters.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>As the war ended and American <a href="/wiki/Lend_Lease" class="mw-redirect" title="Lend Lease">Lend Lease</a> suddenly and unexpectedly ended,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> the Treasury was near bankruptcy and Labour\'s new programmes would be expensive.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The economy did not reach prewar levels until the 1950s. The immediate post-war years were called the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Austerity" class="mw-redirect" title="Age of Austerity">Age of Austerity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKynaston2010_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKynaston2010-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The war almost bankrupted Britain, while the country maintained a global empire in an attempt to remain a global power.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> For instance, it operated a large air force and a conscript army.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Without Lend Lease, bankruptcy loomed. The government secured <a href="/wiki/Anglo-American_loan" title="Anglo-American loan">a low-interest $3.75 billion loan</a> from the US in December 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Rebuilding necessitated fiscal austerity in order to maximise export earnings, while Britain\'s colonies and other client states were required to keep their reserves in pounds as "sterling balances".<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> An additional $3.2 billions of dollars \xe2\x80\x93 which did not have to be repaid \xe2\x80\x93 came from the American <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> in 1948\xe2\x80\x9352. However the Plan did require Britain to modernise its business practices and remove trade barriers. Britain was an enthusiastic supporter of the Marshall Plan, and used it as a lever to more directly promote European unity.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Britain was an enthusiastic cofounder of the <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> military alliance against the Soviets, which was formed in 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan1985269\xe2\x80\x9377_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan1985269\xe2\x80\x9377-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom">Rationing</a>, especially of food, continued in the post-war years as the government tried to control demand and normalise the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>  Anxieties were heightened when the country suffered <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_1946%E2%80%9347_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Winter of 1946\xe2\x80\x9347 in the United Kingdom">one of the worst winters on record in 1946\xe2\x80\x9347</a>: the coal and railway systems failed, factories closed, and a large proportion of the population suffered due to the cold.<sup id="cite_ref-Robertson1989_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Robertson1989-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationing">Rationing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rationing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Wartime_rationing" class="mw-redirect" title="Wartime rationing">Wartime rationing</a> continued, and was for the first time extended to bread in order to feed the German civilians in the British sector of occupied Germany.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> During the war, the government had banned ice cream and rationed sweets, such as chocolates and confections; all sweets were rationed until 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Most people grumbled, but for the poorest, rationing was beneficial, because their rationed diet was of greater nutritional value than their pre-war diet. Housewives organised to oppose the austerity.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives gained support by attacking <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>, austerity, rationing and economic controls and returned to power in 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Morale was boosted by the <a href="/wiki/Wedding_of_Princess_Elizabeth_and_Philip_Mountbatten,_Duke_of_Edinburgh" class="mw-redirect" title="Wedding of Princess Elizabeth and Philip Mountbatten, Duke of Edinburgh">marriage of Princess Elizabeth to Philip Mountbatten</a> in 1947,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKynaston2010&#91;httpswwwcambridgeorgcoreservicesaop-cambridge-corecontentview91DC682C33E4C5C860FFC429789C7EF5S0095139000019955apdfa-tonic-to-the-nation-the-festival-of-britain-1951pdf_445-453&#93;_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKynaston2010[httpswwwcambridgeorgcoreservicesaop-cambridge-corecontentview91DC682C33E4C5C860FFC429789C7EF5S0095139000019955apdfa-tonic-to-the-nation-the-festival-of-britain-1951pdf_445-453]-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/1948_Summer_Olympics" title="1948 Summer Olympics">1948 Summer Olympics</a> were held in London.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Reconstruction had begun in London, but no funding was available for new facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Welfare_state">Welfare state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Welfare state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1948_6d_NI_stamp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/1948_6d_NI_stamp.jpg/220px-1948_6d_NI_stamp.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/1948_6d_NI_stamp.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="309" data-file-height="244" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1948_6d_NI_stamp.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A British 1948 National Insurance stamp, which workers had to purchase to contribute to benefits and pensions</div></div></div>\n<p>The most important Labour initiatives were the expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>, the founding of the <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nationalisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Nationalisation">nationalisation</a> of the coal, gas, electricity, railways and other primary industries. The welfare state was expanded by the <a href="/wiki/National_Insurance_Act_1946" title="National Insurance Act 1946">National Insurance Act 1946</a>, which built upon the comprehensive system of social security originally set up in 1911.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> People of working age had to pay a weekly contribution (by buying a stamp) and in return were entitled to a wide range of benefits, including a pension, health and unemployment benefits, and widows\' benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> began operations in July 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> It promised to give <i>cradle to grave</i> free hospital and medical care for everyone in the country, regardless of income. Labour went on to expand low cost <a href="/wiki/Council_housing" class="mw-redirect" title="Council housing">council housing</a> for the poor.<sup id="cite_ref-Tomlinson2002_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tomlinson2002-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Treasury, headed by Chancellor of the Exchequer <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Dalton" title="Hugh Dalton">Hugh Dalton</a>, faced urgent problems. Half of the wartime economy had been devoted to mobilising soldiers, warplanes, bombs and munitions; a transition to a peacetime budget was begun, while attempting to control inflation.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> New loans from the US and Canada to replace Lend Lease were essential to sustain living conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing">Housing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Housing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Housing was a critical shortage.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Air raids had destroyed half a million housing units; upgrades and repairs on undamaged units had been postponed.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Three-quarters of a million new dwellings were needed.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The government aimed for 300,000 annually,<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> compared to the maximum prewar rate of 350,000. However, shortages of builders, materials,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and money limited progress.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Not counting 150,000 temporary prefabricated units, the shortage reached 1,500,000 units by 1951.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Legislation kept rents down,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> but did not lead to an increase in the number of new homes. The ambitious New Towns project did not provide enough units.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives made housing a high priority and oversaw the building of 2,500,000 new units, two-thirds of them through local councils. Haste made for dubious quality, and policy increasingly shifted towards renovation of existing properties rather than the construction of new ones. Slums were cleared, opening the way for gentrification in the inner cities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnett1986_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnett1986-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationalisation">Nationalisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Nationalisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_nationalizations_by_country#United_Kingdom" title="List of nationalizations by country">List of nationalizations by country \xc2\xa7&#160;United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Martin_Francis" title="Martin Francis">Martin Francis</a> argues there was Labour Party consensus by 1945, both on the <a href="/wiki/National_Executive_Committee" title="National Executive Committee">National Executive Committee</a> and at <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Party (UK) Conference">party conferences</a>, on a definition of socialism that stressed moral as well as material improvement. The Attlee government was committed to rebuilding British society as an ethical commonwealth, using public ownership and controls to abolish extremes of wealth and poverty. Labour\'s ideology contrasted sharply with the contemporary Conservative Party\'s defence of individualism, allowing people to keep much of the wealth they had created, inherited privileges and income inequality.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Attlee\'s government nationalised major industries and utilities. It developed and implemented the "cradle to grave" <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a> conceived by liberal economist <a href="/wiki/William_Beveridge" title="William Beveridge">William Beveridge</a>. The creation of Britain\'s publicly funded <a href="/wiki/National_Health_Service" title="National Health Service">National Health Service</a> under health minister <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a> remains Labour\'s proudest achievement.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the Labour Party had developed no detailed plans nationalization plans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkedCook197929_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkedCook197929-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (May 2018)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Improvising, they started with the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_England" title="Bank of England">Bank of England</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_European_Airways" title="British European Airways">civil aviation</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Coal_Board" title="National Coal Board">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cable_and_Wireless" class="mw-redirect" title="Cable and Wireless">Cable and Wireless</a>. Then came <a href="/wiki/British_Rail" title="British Rail">railways</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Transport_Commission" title="British Transport Commission">canals</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Freight_Corporation" title="National Freight Corporation">road haulage and trucking</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Electricity_Authority" title="British Electricity Authority">electricity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gas_board" title="Gas board">gas</a>. Finally came <a href="/wiki/Iron_and_Steel_Corporation_of_Great_Britain" title="Iron and Steel Corporation of Great Britain">iron and steel</a>, which was a special case because it was a manufacturing industry. Altogether, about one fifth of the economy was taken over. Labour dropped the notion of nationalising farms.\n</p><p>On the whole nationalisation went smoothly, with two exceptions. Nationalising hospitals was strongly opposed by practising physicians. Compromises allowed them also to have a private practice, and the great majority decided to work with the National Health Service. Much more controversial was the nationalisation of the iron and steel industry \xe2\x80\x94 unlike coal, it was profitable and highly efficient. Nationalisation was opposed by industry owners and executives, the business community as a whole and the Conservative Party as a whole. The <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> was also opposed, but the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_Act_1949" title="Parliament Act 1949">Parliament Act 1949</a> reduced its power to delay legislation to just one year. Finally in 1951, iron and steel were nationalised, but then Labour lost its majority. The Conservatives in 1955 returned them to private ownership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkedCook1979Chapters_2-4_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkedCook1979Chapters_2-4-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (May 2018)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>The procedure used was developed by <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Morrison" title="Herbert Morrison">Herbert Morrison</a>, who as <a href="/wiki/Lord_President_of_the_Council" title="Lord President of the Council">Lord President of the Council</a> chaired the Committee on the Socialisation of Industries. He followed the model that was already in place of setting up public corporations such as the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> in broadcasting (1927). The owners of corporate stock were given government bonds, and the government took full ownership of each affected company, consolidating it into a national monopoly. The managers remained the same, only now they became civil servants working for the government. For the Labour Party leadership, nationalisation was a way to consolidate economic planning. It was not designed to modernise old industries, make them efficient, or transform their organisational structure. There was no money for modernisation, although the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>, operated separately by American planners, did force many British businesses to adopt modern managerial techniques. Hard line British Marxists were fervent believers in <a href="/wiki/Dialectical_materialism" title="Dialectical materialism">dialectical materialism</a> and in fighting against <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> and for <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_control" title="Workers&#39; control">workers\' control</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trade_unionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Trade unionism">trade unionism</a>, nationalisation of industry and centralized planning. They were now disappointed, as the nationalised industries seemed identical to the old private corporations, and national planning was made virtually impossible by the government\'s financial constraints. At Oxford a "<a href="/wiki/New_Left" title="New Left">New Left</a>" started to emerge that rejected old-line approaches.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Socialism was in place, but it did not seem to make a major difference. Rank-and-file workers had long been motivated to support Labour by tales of the mistreatment of workers by foremen and management. The foremen and the managers were the same people as before, with much the same power over the workplace. There was no worker control of industry. The unions resisted government efforts to set wages. By the time of the general elections in 1950 and 1951, Labour seldom boasted about its nationalisations. Instead Conservatives decried the inefficiency and mismanagement, and promised to reverse the treatment of steel and trucking.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESkedCook197931\xe2\x80\x9334_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESkedCook197931\xe2\x80\x9334-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (May 2018)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beer1966_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beer1966-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour_weaknesses">Labour weaknesses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Labour weaknesses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Labour struggled to maintain its support. Realising the unpopularity of rationing, in 1948\xe2\x80\x9349 the government ended the rationing of potatoes, bread, shoes, clothing and jam, and increased the petrol ration for summer drivers. However, meat was still rationed, and in very short supply, at high prices.<sup id="cite_ref-Medlicott1967_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Medlicott1967-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Militant socialist <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a>, the Minister of Health, said at a party rally in 1948, "no amount of cajolery... can eradicate from my heart a deep burning hatred for the Tory Party.... They are lower than vermin." Bevan, a coal miner\'s son, had gone too far in a land that took pride in self-restraint, and he never lived down the remark.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKynaston2010284_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKynaston2010284-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Labour narrowly won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1950" title="United Kingdom general election, 1950">1950 general election</a> with a majority of five seats. Defence became one of the divisive issues for Labour itself, especially defence spending, which reached 14% of GDP in 1951 during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>. These costs strained public finances. The <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a>, introduced <a href="/wiki/Prescription_charges" title="Prescription charges">prescription charges</a> for NHS <a href="/wiki/Dentures" title="Dentures">dentures</a> and spectacles, leading Bevan, along with <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> (<a href="/wiki/President_of_the_Board_of_Trade" title="President of the Board of Trade">President of the Board of Trade</a>) to resign. A decade of turmoil ensued in the Party, much to the advantage of the Conservatives who won again and again by ever larger majorities.<sup id="cite_ref-Foot2011_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foot2011-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/David_Kynaston" title="David Kynaston">David Kynaston</a> argues that the Labour Party under Attlee was led by conservative parliamentarians who always worked through constitutional parliamentary channels; they saw no need for large demonstrations, boycotts or symbolic strikes. The result was a solid expansion and coordination of the welfare system, most notably the concentrated and centralised NHS. Private sector nationalisation focused on older, declining industries, most notably coal mining. Labour kept promising systematic economic planning, but never established adequate mechanisms. Much of the planning was forced upon them by the Marshall Plan, which insisted on a modernisation of business procedures and government regulations.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The Keynesian model accepted by Labour emphasised that planning could be handled indirectly through national spending and tax policies.<sup id="cite_ref-Pugh2017_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pugh2017-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cold_War">Cold War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cold War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Britain faced severe financial constraints, lacking cash for needed imports. It responded by reducing its international entanglements as in Greece, and by sharing the hardships of an "age of austerity."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKynaston2010Chapter_4_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKynaston2010Chapter_4-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Early fears that the US would veto nationalisation or welfare policies proved groundless.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under Attlee foreign policy was the domain of <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Bevin" title="Ernest Bevin">Ernest Bevin</a>, who looked for innovative ways to bring western Europe together in a military alliance. One early attempt was the <a href="/wiki/Dunkirk_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunkirk Treaty">Dunkirk Treaty</a> with France in 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Bevin\'s commitment to the West European security system made him eager to sign the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brussels" title="Treaty of Brussels">Treaty of Brussels</a> in 1948. It drew Britain, France, Belgium, the Netherlands and Luxembourg into an arrangement for collective security, opening the way for the formation of <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> in 1949. NATO was primarily aimed as a defensive measure against Soviet expansion, while helping bring its members closer together and enabled them to modernise their forces along parallel lines, also encouraging arms purchases from Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Bevin began the process of dismantling the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> when it granted independence to India and Pakistan in 1947, followed by Burma (Myanmar) and Ceylon (Sri Lanka) in 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-Brendon2008_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brendon2008-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> In January 1947, the government decided to proceed with the development of Britain\'s <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom" title="Nuclear weapons and the United Kingdom">nuclear weapons programme</a>, primarily to enhance Britain\'s security and also its status as a superpower. A handful of top elected officials made the decision in secret, ignoring the rest of the cabinet, in order to forestall the Labour Party\'s pacifist and anti-nuclear wing.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Return_of_Churchill">Return of Churchill</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Return of Churchill">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the late 1940s the Conservative Party exploited and incited growing public anger at <a href="/wiki/Rationing_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Rationing in the United Kingdom">food rationing</a>, scarcity, controls, austerity and omnipresent government bureaucracy. They used the dissatisfaction with their opponent\'s socialist and egalitarian policies to rally middle-class supporters and score a political victory at the 1951 general election. Their appeal was especially effective to housewives, who faced more difficult shopping conditions after the war than during it.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:In16695.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/In16695.jpg/220px-In16695.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/In16695.jpg/330px-In16695.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/In16695.jpg/440px-In16695.jpg 2x" data-file-width="477" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:In16695.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 300-foot Skylon hung in midair at the Festival of Britain, 1951</div></div></div>\n<p>The Labour Party kept slipping, interrupted by good moments such as the <a href="/wiki/Festival_of_Britain" title="Festival of Britain">Festival of Britain</a> in summer 1951, a national exhibition and fair held throughout the country. Historian <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O._Morgan" title="Kenneth O. Morgan">Kenneth O. Morgan</a> says the Festival was a "triumphant success" as every day thousands:\n</p>\n<dl><dd>flocked to the South Bank site [in London], to wander around the <a href="/wiki/Dome_of_Discovery" title="Dome of Discovery">Dome of Discovery</a>, gaze at the <a href="/wiki/Skylon_(Festival_of_Britain)" title="Skylon (Festival of Britain)">Skylon</a>, and generally enjoy a festival of national celebration. Up and down the land, lesser festivals enlisted much civic and voluntary enthusiasm. A people curbed by years of total war and half-crushed by austerity and gloom, showed that it had not lost the capacity for enjoying itself....Above all, the Festival made a spectacular setting as a showpiece for the inventiveness and genius of British scientists and technologists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan1992110_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan1992110-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>The Conservative Party restored its credibility on economic policy with the <i><a href="/wiki/Industrial_Charter" title="Industrial Charter">Industrial Charter</a></i> written by <a href="/wiki/Rab_Butler" title="Rab Butler">Rab Butler</a>, which emphasised the importance of removing unnecessary controls, while going far beyond the laissez-faire attitude of old towards industrial social problems. Churchill was party leader, but he brought in a Party Chairman to modernise the institution. <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Marquis,_1st_Earl_of_Woolton" title="Frederick Marquis, 1st Earl of Woolton">Lord Woolton</a> was a successful department store owner and wartime <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Food" title="Minister of Food">Minister of Food</a>. As Party Chairman 1946\xe2\x80\x9355, he rebuilt its local organisations with an emphasis on membership, money and a unified national propaganda appeal on critical issues. To broaden the base of potential candidates, the national party provided financial aid and assisted local organisations in raising local money. Lord Woolton emphasised a rhetoric that characterised the opponents as "Socialist" rather than "Labour." The <a href="/wiki/Libertarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Libertarian">libertarian</a> influence of Professor <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a>\'s 1944 best-seller <i>Road to Serfdom</i> was apparent in the younger generation, but that took another quarter century to have a policy impact. By 1951, Labour\'s factions were bitterly divided.\n</p><p>The Conservatives narrowly won the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1951" title="United Kingdom general election, 1951">October 1951 election</a>, although Labour got considerably more votes. Most of the new programmes passed by Labour were accepted by the Conservatives and became part of the "<a href="/wiki/Post_war_consensus" class="mw-redirect" title="Post war consensus">post war consensus</a>" that lasted until the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The Conservatives ended rationing and reduced controls, and sold the famous <a href="/wiki/Skylon_(Festival_of_Britain)" title="Skylon (Festival of Britain)">Skylon</a> for scrap. They were conciliatory towards unions and retained nationalisation and the welfare state, while privatising the steel and road haulage industries in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan1992114_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan1992114-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_Age">Golden Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Golden Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In the 1950s, rebuilding continued, and immigrants from Commonwealth nations, mostly from the Caribbean and the Indian subcontinent, began arriving in a steady flow. The shock of the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> of 1956 made clear that Britain had lost its role as a <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpower</a>. It already knew it could no longer afford its large <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">Empire</a>. This led to decolonisation, and a withdrawal from almost all of its colonies by 1970.\n</p><p>In 1957 Prime Minister Macmillan boasted:<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Let us be frank about it: most of our people have never had it so good. Go round the country, go to the industrial towns, go to the farms and you will see a state of prosperity such as we have never had in my lifetime \xe2\x80\x93 nor indeed in the history of this country.\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Unemployment figures<sup id="cite_ref-SlomanGarratt2015_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SlomanGarratt2015-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> show that unemployment was significantly lower during the Golden Age than before or after:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Epoch</th>\n<th>Date range</th>\n<th>% of British labour force unemployed\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Pre\xe2\x80\x93Golden Age</td>\n<td>1921\xe2\x80\x931938</td>\n<td>13.4\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Golden Age</td>\n<td>1950\xe2\x80\x931969</td>\n<td>1.6\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Post\xe2\x80\x93Golden Age</td>\n<td>1970\xe2\x80\x931993</td>\n<td>6.7\n</td></tr>\n</tbody></table>\n<p>In addition to superior economic performance, other social indexes were higher in the golden age; for example, the proportion of Britain\'s population saying they are "very happy" registered at 52% in 1957 bu fell to just 36% in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Healey2002_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Healey2002-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 1950s and 1960s experienced continued modernisation of the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-BlackPemberton2017_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlackPemberton2017-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Representative was the construction of the first <a href="/wiki/Motorway" class="mw-redirect" title="Motorway">motorways</a>. Britain maintained and increased its financial role in the world economy, and used the English language to promote its educational system to students from around the globe. With relatively low unemployment during this period, the standard of living continued to rise, with new private and council housing developments increasing and the number of slum properties diminishing.\n</p><p>During the period, unemployment in Britain averaged only 2%. As prosperity returned after the war, Britons became more family-centred.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKynaston2009_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKynaston2009-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Leisure activities became more accessible to more people. <a href="/wiki/Holiday_camp" title="Holiday camp">Holiday camps</a>, which had first opened in the 1930s, became popular holiday destinations in the 1950s \xe2\x80\x93 and people increasingly had the ability to pursue personal hobbies. The <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a>\'s early television service was given a major boost in 1953 with the coronation of <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabeth II of the United Kingdom">Elizabeth II</a>, attracting a worldwide audience of twenty million, plus tens of millions more by radio. Many middle-class people bought televisions to view the event. In 1950 just 1% owned television sets; by 1965 25% did, and many more were rented. As austerity receded after 1950 and consumer demand kept growing, the Labour Party hurt itself by shunning consumerism as the antithesis of the socialism it demanded.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Small neighbourhood shops were increasingly replaced by <a href="/wiki/Chain_store" title="Chain store">chain stores</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shopping_centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Shopping centre">shopping centres</a>. Cars were becoming a significant part of British life, with city-centre <a href="/wiki/Traffic_congestion" title="Traffic congestion">congestion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ribbon_development" title="Ribbon development">ribbon developments</a> springing up along major roads. These problems led to the idea of a <a href="/wiki/Green_belt" title="Green belt">green belt</a> to protect the countryside, which was at risk from development of new housing units.<sup id="cite_ref-Vliet--1987_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vliet--1987-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The post-war period witnessed a dramatic rise in the average standard of living, with a 40% rise in average real wages from 1950 to 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAddisonJones2008_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAddisonJones2008-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Workers in traditionally poorly paid semi-skilled and unskilled occupations saw a particularly marked improvement in their wages and living standards. Consumption, became more equal, especially as the landed gentry was pressed to pay its taxes and had to reduce its level of consumption. The rise in wages spurred consumer spending to increase by about 20% during the period, while economic growth continued at about 3%. The last food rations were ended in 1954, along with hire-purchase controls. As a result of these changes, large numbers of the working classes were able to participate in the consumer market for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The number one major purchase was a <a href="/wiki/Washing_machine" title="Washing machine">washing machine</a>. Ownership jumped from 18 percent in 1955 to 29 percent in 1958 and 60 percent in 1966.<sup id="cite_ref-Sandbrook2015_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sandbrook2015-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\t\n</p><p>Various fringe benefits became more common. In 1955, 96% of manual labourers were entitled to two weeks\' holiday with pay, compared with 61% in 1951. By the end of the 1950s, Britain had become one of the world\'s most affluent countries, and by the early Sixties, most Britons enjoyed a level of prosperity that had previously been the privilege of only a small minority.<sup id="cite_ref-Hill1985_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hill1985-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> For the first time in decades, the young and unattached had spare cash for leisure, clothes and even luxuries. In 1959, <i><a href="/wiki/Queen_(magazine)" title="Queen (magazine)">Queen</a></i> magazine declared that "Britain has launched into an age of unparalleled lavish living." Average wages were high while jobs were plentiful, and people saw their personal prosperity climb even higher. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a> claimed that "the luxuries of the rich have become the necessities of the poor." As summed up by <a href="/wiki/R._J._Unstead" title="R. J. Unstead">R. J. Unstead</a>,\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Opportunities in life, if not equal, were distributed much more fairly than ever before and the weekly wage-earner, in particular, had gained standards of living that would have been almost unbelievable in the thirties.<sup id="cite_ref-Unstead1967_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Unstead1967-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Labour historian <a href="/wiki/Martin_Pugh_(author)" class="mw-redirect" title="Martin Pugh (author)">Martin Pugh</a> stated:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Keynesian economic management enabled British workers to enjoy a golden age of full employment which, combined with a more relaxed attitude towards working mothers, led to the spread of the two-income family. Inflation was around 4 per cent, money wages rose from an average of \xc2\xa38 a week in 1951 to \xc2\xa315 a week by 1961, home-ownership spread from 35 per cent in 1939 to 47 per cent by 1966, and the relaxation of credit controls boosted the demand for consumer goods.<sup id="cite_ref-Pugh2011_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pugh2011-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>By 1963, 82% of all private households had a television, 72% a vacuum cleaner, 45% a washing machine, and 30% a refrigerator. John Burnett notes that ownership had spread down the social scale so that the gap between consumption by professional and manual workers had considerably narrowed. The provision of household amenities steadily improved in the late decades of the century. From 1971-1983, households having the sole use of a fixed bath or shower rose from 88% to 97%, and those with an indoor toilet from 87% to 97%. In addition, the number of households with central heating almost doubled during that same period, from 34% to 64%. By 1983, 94% of all households had a refrigerator, 81% a colour television, 80% a washing machine, 57% a deep freezer, and 28% a tumble-drier.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnett1986302_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnett1986302-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From a European perspective, however, the UK was not keeping pace. Between 1950-1970, it was overtaken by most of the countries of the European Common Market in terms of telephones, refrigerators, television sets, cars and washing machines per household.<sup id="cite_ref-Lapping1970_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lapping1970-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Education grew, but not as fast as in rival nations. By the early-1980s, some 80% to 90% of school leavers in France and West Germany received vocational training, compared with 40% in the United Kingdom. By the mid-1980s, over 80% of pupils in the United States and West Germany and over 90% in Japan stayed in education until the age of eighteen, compared with barely 33% of British pupils.<sup id="cite_ref-MacDowall2000_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacDowall2000-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> In 1987, only 35% of 16-18-year-olds<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(geographic_names)" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (geographic names)"><span title="The geographic scope near this tag is ambiguous. (January 2017)">where?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> were in full-time education or training, compared with 80% in the United States, 77% in Japan, 69% in France, and 49% in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s_economic_crises">1970s economic crises</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1970s economic crises">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Three-Day_Week" title="Three-Day Week">Three-Day Week</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a></div>\n<p>In comparing economic prosperity (using gross national product per person), the British record was one of steady downward slippage from seventh place in 1950, to twelfth in 1965, to twentieth in 1975. Labour politician <a href="/wiki/Richard_Crossman" title="Richard Crossman">Richard Crossman</a>, after visiting prosperous <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, returned to England with a\n</p>\n<dl><dd>sense of restriction, yes, even of decline, the old country always teetering on the edge of a crisis, trying to keep up appearances, with no confident vision of the future.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011305_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011305-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<p>Economists provided four overlapping explanations. The "early start" theory said that Britain\'s rivals were doing so well because they were still moving large numbers of farm workers into more lucrative employment, which Britain had done in the nineteenth century. A second theory emphasised the "rejuvenation by defeat", whereby Germany and Japan had been forced to re-equip, rethink and restructure their economies. The third approach emphasised the drag of "imperial distractions", saying that responsibilities to its large empire handicapped the home economy, especially through defence spending, and economic aid. Finally, the theory of "institutional failure" stressed the negative roles of discontinuity, unpredictability, and class envy. The last theory blamed trade unions, public schools, and universities for perpetuating an elitist anti-industrial attitude.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011305-6_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011305-6-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1970s, the exuberance and the radicalism of the 1960s ebbed. Instead a mounting series of economic crises, including many trade union strikes, pushed the British economy further and further behind European and world growth. The result was a major political crisis, and a <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a> in the winter of 1978\xe2\x80\x9379, when widespread strikes by public sector trade unions seriously inconvenienced and angered the public.<sup id="cite_ref-Turner2009_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2009-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Beckett2009_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckett2009-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Historians <a href="/wiki/Alan_Sked" title="Alan Sked">Alan Sked</a> and Chris Cook summarise the general consensus of historians regarding Labour in power in the 1970s:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If Wilson\'s record as prime minister was soon felt to have been one of failure, that sense of failure was powerfully reinforced by <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">Callaghan</a>\'s term as premier. Labour, it seemed, was incapable of positive achievements. It was unable to control inflation, unable to control the unions, unable to solve the Irish problem, unable to solve the Rhodesian question, unable to secure its proposals for Welsh and Scottish devolution, unable to reach a popular <i>modus vivendi</i> with the Common Market, unable even to maintain itself in power until it could go to the country and the date of its own choosing. It was little wonder, therefore, that Mrs. Thatcher resoundingly defeated it in 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESked1979324_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESked1979324-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (May 2018)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Bright spots included large deposits of oil that were found in the North Sea, allowing Britain to become a major oil exporter to Europe in the era of the <a href="/wiki/1970s_energy_crisis" title="1970s energy crisis">1970s energy crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BendingEden1984_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BendingEden1984-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_term_economic_factors">Long term economic factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Long term economic factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While economic historians concentrate on statistical parameters, cultural historians added to the list of factors to explain Britain\'s long-term relative economic decline. According to <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hennessy" title="Peter Hennessy">Peter Hennessy</a>, these include:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Excessive trade union power.</li>\n<li>Too much nationalisation.</li>\n<li>Insufficient entrepreneurship.</li>\n<li>Too many wars, both hot and cold.</li>\n<li>The distraction of imperialism.</li>\n<li>A feeble political class.</li>\n<li>A weak civil service</li>\n<li>An enduring aristocratic tradition disparaged management.</li>\n<li>Weak vocational education at all levels.</li>\n<li>Social class rigidities interence with progress.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2018)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Ireland_and_the_Troubles">Northern Ireland and the Troubles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Northern Ireland and the Troubles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Troubles" title="The Troubles">The Troubles</a></div>\n<p>In the 1960s, moderate Unionist <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Northern_Ireland" title="Prime Minister of Northern Ireland">Prime Minister of Northern Ireland</a> <a href="/wiki/Terence_O%27Neill,_Baron_O%27Neill_of_the_Maine" class="mw-redirect" title="Terence O&#39;Neill, Baron O&#39;Neill of the Maine">Terence O\'Neill</a> tried to reform the system and give a greater voice to Catholics, who comprised 40% of the population of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a>. His goals were blocked by militant Protestants led by Reverend <a href="/wiki/Ian_Paisley" title="Ian Paisley">Ian Paisley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mulholland2000_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mulholland2000-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The increasing pressures from nationalists for reform and from unionists for "No surrender" led to the appearance of the civil rights movement under figures such as <a href="/wiki/John_Hume" title="John Hume">John Hume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austin_Currie" title="Austin Currie">Austin Currie</a>. Clashes escalated out of control, as the army could barely contain the <a href="/wiki/Provisional_Irish_Republican_Army" title="Provisional Irish Republican Army">Provisional Irish Republican Army</a> (IRA) and the <a href="/wiki/Ulster_Defence_Association" title="Ulster Defence Association">Ulster Defence Association</a>. British leaders feared their withdrawal would lead to a "doomsday scenario", with widespread communal strife, followed by the mass exodus of hundreds of thousands of refugees. The UK Parliament in London shut down Northern Ireland\'s parliament and imposed <a href="/wiki/Direct_rule_over_Northern_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Direct rule over Northern Ireland">direct rule</a>. By the 1990s, the failure of the IRA campaign to win mass public support or achieve its aim of a British withdrawal led to negotiations that in 1998 produced  what is commonly referred to as the \'<a href="/wiki/Good_Friday_Agreement" title="Good Friday Agreement">Good Friday Agreement</a>\'. This won popular support and largely ended the most violent aspects of The Troubles.<sup id="cite_ref-Dixon2008_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon2008-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Farrington2006_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Farrington2006-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_cultural_forces">Social and cultural forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Social and cultural forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Princess_Diana">Princess Diana</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Princess Diana">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wedding_of_Charles,_Prince_of_Wales,_and_Lady_Diana_Spencer" class="mw-redirect" title="Wedding of Charles, Prince of Wales, and Lady Diana Spencer">Wedding of Charles, Prince of Wales, and Lady Diana Spencer</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diana,_Princess_of_Wales.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Diana%2C_Princess_of_Wales.jpg/220px-Diana%2C_Princess_of_Wales.jpg" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Diana%2C_Princess_of_Wales.jpg/330px-Diana%2C_Princess_of_Wales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Diana%2C_Princess_of_Wales.jpg/440px-Diana%2C_Princess_of_Wales.jpg 2x" data-file-width="461" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diana,_Princess_of_Wales.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Diana, Princess of Wales">Diana, Princess of Wales</a> was married to <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Prince_of_Wales" title="Charles, Prince of Wales">Charles, Prince of Wales</a> from 1981 to 1996. She died in a <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Death of Diana, Princess of Wales">car accident in 1997</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>During the summer of 1981, the nation\'s spirits were raised by the wedding of <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Charles">Prince Charles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lady_Diana_Spencer" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Diana Spencer">Lady Diana Spencer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies2001_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2001-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The ceremony reached a global TV audience of 750 million people. It restored the royal family to the headlines where they would become a permanent fixture in tabloids and celebrity gossip publications, as well as a major tourist attraction. Diana became what Tony Blair called the "People\'s Princess," an iconic national figure, rivalling or surpassing the Queen, until her divorce. <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Diana,_Princess_of_Wales" title="Death of Diana, Princess of Wales">Her accidental death</a> brought an unprecedented spasm of grief and mourning.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeight2002659,_681_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeight2002659,_681-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (May 2018)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Her brother, the <a href="/wiki/Charles_Spencer,_9th_Earl_Spencer" title="Charles Spencer, 9th Earl Spencer">9th Earl Spencer</a>, captured her role:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>Diana was the very essence of compassion, of duty, of style, of beauty. All over the world she was a symbol of selfless humanity. All over the world, a standard bearer for the rights of the truly downtrodden, a very British girl who transcended nationality. Someone with a natural nobility who was classless and who proved in the last year that she needed no royal title to continue to generate her particular brand of magic.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Secularisation">Secularisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Secularisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity_in_Britain" title="History of Christianity in Britain">History of Christianity in Britain</a></div>\n<p>In the late 1940s Britain was still a Christian nation, with its religiosity reinforced by the wartime experience. Peter Forster found that in answering pollsters, the British people reported an overwhelming belief in the truth of Christianity, a high respect for it, and a strong association between it and moral behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hennessy" title="Peter Hennessy">Peter Hennessy</a> argued that long-held attitudes did not stop change; by mid-century "Britain was still a Christian country only in a vague attitudinal sense, belief generally being more a residual husk than the kernel of conviction."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHennessy2006_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHennessy2006-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_O._Morgan" title="Kenneth O. Morgan">Kenneth O. Morgan</a> agreed, noting that "the Protestant churches, Anglican, and more especially non-conformist, all felt the pressure of falling numbers and of secular challenges....Even the drab Sabbath of Wales and Scotland was under some threat, with pressure for opening cinemas in Wales and golf-courses in Scotland."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorgan1985299_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorgan1985299-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Brian_Harrison_(historian)" title="Brian Harrison (historian)">Brian Harrison</a> reports that the forces of secularisation grew rapidly, and by the 1990s Protestantism was a shadow of its 1945 strength. Compared to Western Europe, Britain stood at the lower end of attendance at religious services, and near the top in people claiming "no religion". While 80% of Britons in 1950 said they were Christians, only 64% did so in 2000. Harrison states:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>By every measure (number of churches, number of parish clergy, church attendance, Easter Day communicants, number of church marriages, membership as a proportion of the adult population) the Church of England was in decline after 1970. In 1985 there were only half as many parish clergy as in 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2009371-72_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2009371-72-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Roman Catholicism held up, thanks initially to immigration from Ireland and later from Poland.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> What had been tiny clusters of Muslims, Hindus, Sikhs and Buddhists grew enormously through immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011339-48_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011339-48-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2009373-81_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2009373-81-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_trends">Growth trends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Growth trends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<table class="mw-datatable floatright">\n\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Population of religious groups in Britain<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECookStevenson2014143_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECookStevenson2014143-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</th>\n<th>1970\n</th>\n<th>1993\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Jews</td>\n<td>450,000</td>\n<td>300,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Muslim</td>\n<td>250,000</td>\n<td>1,000,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Sikh</td>\n<td>75,000</td>\n<td>300,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Hindu</td>\n<td>50,000</td>\n<td>320,000\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Buddhist</td>\n<td>6,000</td>\n<td>25,000\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The Muslim population of England and Wales was over 50 times larger in 2011 compared to 50 years before. Sophie Gilliat-Ray attributes the growth to "recent immigration, the growing birth rate, some conversion to Islam, and perhaps also an increased willingness to self-identify as \'Muslim\' on account of the \'war on terror\'."<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="mw-datatable" style="width:100%">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Census year</th>\n<th>Number of Muslims<sup id="cite_ref-BRIN_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BRIN-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></th>\n<th>Population of England &amp; Wales</th>\n<th>% of population</th>\n<th>Registered mosques\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1961</td>\n<td>50,000</td>\n<td>46,196,000</td>\n<td>0.11%</td>\n<td>7\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1971</td>\n<td>226,000</td>\n<td>49,152,000</td>\n<td>0.46%</td>\n<td>30\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1981</td>\n<td>553,000</td>\n<td>49,634,000</td>\n<td>1.11%</td>\n<td>149\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1991</td>\n<td>950,000</td>\n<td>51,099,000</td>\n<td>1.86%</td>\n<td>443\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2001</td>\n<td>1,600,000</td>\n<td>52,042,000</td>\n<td>3.07%</td>\n<td>614\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2011</td>\n<td>2,706,000</td>\n<td>56,076,000</td>\n<td>4.83</td>\n<td>1,500\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_women">Status of women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Status of women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_women_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="History of women in the United Kingdom">History of women in the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feminism_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Feminism in the United Kingdom">Feminism in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The 1950s was a bleak period for <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">feminism</a>. In the aftermath of World War II, a new emphasis was placed on companionate marriage and the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">nuclear family</a> as a foundation of the new <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Smith1990_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith1990-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1951, the proportion of adult women who were (or had been) married was 75%;
