more specifically, 84.8% of women between the ages of 45 and 49 were married.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis1984_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis1984-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> At that time: "marriage was more popular than ever before."<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In 1953, a popular book of advice for women states: "A happy marriage may be seen, not as a holy state or something to which a few may luckily attain, but rather as the best course, the simplest, and the easiest way of life for us all".<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While at the end of the war, childcare facilities were closed and assistance for working women became limited, the social reforms implemented by the new welfare state included <a href="/wiki/Family_Allowances_Act_1945" title="Family Allowances Act 1945">family allowances</a> meant to subsidise families, that is, to support women in their "capacity as wife and mother."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Sue Bruley argues that "the progressive vision of the New Britain of 1945 was flawed by a fundamentally conservative view of women".<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Women\'s commitment to companionate marriage was echoed by the popular media: films, radio and popular <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_magazines" class="mw-redirect" title="Women&#39;s magazines">women\'s magazines</a>. In the 1950s, women\'s magazines had considerable influence on forming opinion in all walks of life, including the attitude to women\'s employment.\n</p><p>Nevertheless, 1950s Britain moved to <a href="/wiki/Equal_pay_for_women" class="mw-redirect" title="Equal pay for women">equal pay</a> for teachers (1952) and for men and women in the civil service (1954), thanks to activists like <a href="/wiki/Edith_Summerskill" title="Edith Summerskill">Edith Summerskill</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Caine" title="Barbara Caine">Barbara Caine</a> argues: "Ironically here, as with the vote, success was sometimes the worst enemy of organised feminism, as the achievement of each goal brought to an end the campaign which had been organised around it, leaving nothing in its place."<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Feminist writers of the early postwar period, such as <a href="/wiki/Alva_Myrdal" title="Alva Myrdal">Alva Myrdal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viola_Klein" title="Viola Klein">Viola Klein</a>, started to allow for the possibility that women should be able to combine home duties with outside employment. Feminism was strongly connected to social responsibility and involved the well-being of society as a whole. This often came at the cost of the liberation and personal fulfillment of self-declared feminists. Even those women who regarded themselves as feminists strongly endorsed prevailing ideas about the primacy of children\'s needs, as advocated, for example, by <a href="/wiki/John_Bowlby" title="John Bowlby">John Bowlby</a> the head of the Children\'s Department at the <a href="/wiki/Tavistock_Clinic" class="mw-redirect" title="Tavistock Clinic">Tavistock Clinic</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Donald_Winnicott" title="Donald Winnicott">Donald Winnicott</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (May 2018)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Equal pay entered the agenda at the <a href="/wiki/1959_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1959 UK general election">1959 general election</a>, when the Labour Party\'s Manifesto proposed a charter of rights including: "the right to equal pay for equal work". Polls in 1968-9 showed public opinion moving in favour of equal pay for equal work; nearly three-quarters of those polled favoured the principle. The <a href="/wiki/Equal_Pay_Act_1970" title="Equal Pay Act 1970">Equal Pay Act 1970</a> was passed by a Labour government with support from the Conservatives; it took effect in 1975. Women\'s wages for like work rose sharply from 64% in 1970 to 74% by 1980, then stalled because of high unemployment, and public-sector cuts that hit women working part-time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2009233_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2009233-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexuality_in_1960s_and_1970s">Sexuality in 1960s and 1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Sexuality in 1960s and 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In the 1960s, the generations divided sharply regarding sexual freedoms demanded by youth that disrupted long-held norms.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sexual morals changed rapidly. One notable event was the publication of <a href="/wiki/D._H._Lawrence" title="D. H. Lawrence">D. H. Lawrence</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Lady_Chatterley%27s_Lover" title="Lady Chatterley&#39;s Lover">Lady Chatterley\'s Lover</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Penguin_Books" title="Penguin Books">Penguin Books</a> in 1960. Although first printed in 1928, the release in 1960 of an inexpensive paperback prompted <a href="/wiki/R_v_Penguin_Books_Ltd" title="R v Penguin Books Ltd">a court case</a>. The prosecutor\'s question, "Would you want your wife or servants to read this book?" highlighted how far society had changed, and how little some people had noticed. The book was seen as one of the first events in a general relaxation of sexual attitudes. The national media, based in London with its more permissive social norms, led in explaining and exploring the new permissiveness.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other elements of the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_revolution" title="Sexual revolution">sexual revolution</a> included the development of <a href="/wiki/The_Pill" class="mw-redirect" title="The Pill">the contraceptive pill</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Quant" title="Mary Quant">Mary Quant</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Miniskirt" title="Miniskirt">miniskirt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_Offences_Act_1967" title="Sexual Offences Act 1967">partial decriminalisation</a> of male <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality" title="Homosexuality">homosexuality</a> in 1967. The incidence of divorce and abortion rose along with a resurgence of the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_liberation_movement" title="Women&#39;s liberation movement">women\'s liberation movement</a>, whose campaigning helped secure the <a href="/wiki/Equal_Pay_Act_1970" title="Equal Pay Act 1970">Equal Pay Act 1970</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sex_Discrimination_Act_1975" title="Sex Discrimination Act 1975">Sex Discrimination Act 1975</a>.\n</p><p>Irish Catholics, traditionally the most puritanical of the ethno-religious groups, eased up a little, especially as the membership disregarded the bishops\' teaching that contraception was sinful.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The feminist movement drew inspiration primarily from the United States, and from the experience of left-wing British women experiencing discrimination by male activists. Efforts to form a national movement in the mid-1970s foundered on a bitter split between the (predominantly heterosexual) socialists, and the (predominantly lesbian) radicals. The most visible spokesperson was <a href="/wiki/Germaine_Greer" title="Germaine Greer">Germaine Greer</a>, whose <i><a href="/wiki/The_Female_Eunuch" title="The Female Eunuch">The Female Eunuch</a></i> (1970) called on women to rebel against marriage and instead live in heterosexual communes. <a href="/wiki/Paul_Addison" title="Paul Addison">Paul Addison</a> concludes that," in popular culture, feminism was generally treated as a bit of a joke."<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> received scant credit from feminists for breaking the ultimate glass ceiling, because she avoided feminism, and expressed a strong, independent persona.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Teenagers">Teenagers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Teenagers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>"Teenager" was an American coinage that first appeared in the British social scene in the late-1930s. National attention focused on them from the 1950s onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup>  Improved nutrition across the entire population was causing the age of <a href="/wiki/Menarche" title="Menarche">menarche</a> to fall on average by three or four months every decade, for well over a century. Young people aged between 12-20 were physically much more mature than before. They were better-educated, and their parents had more money.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> National Service\xe2\x80\x94the conscription of young men age 17-21 for compulsory military service-- <a href="/wiki/National_Service_Act_1948" title="National Service Act 1948">was introduced in 1948</a>; when it was abolished in 1960, the young men who would have reached conscription age had eighteen more months of freedom. The widespread use of washing machines, vacuum cleaners, kitchen appliances and prepared foods meant that teenage girls were no longer needed for so many household chores.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The middle and upper-class populations were mostly still enrolled in school, so that much of the teenage phenomena of the postwar years was a product of the working-class. There are two dimensions of special importance, first the economics of teenage consumerism, and secondly; a middle-class moral panic about the decline in British morality. Looking just at the population of unmarried young people between 15-25 years of age, there were 5,000,000 of them in 1960, and they controlled about 10% of all personal income in Britain. They had blue-collar jobs that paid fairly well after the austerity years had ended. They typically lived at home, and did not spend their allowances and wages on housing, groceries, taxes, appliances, furniture or savings for the future. Instead, came the immediate need to urgently keep up with the standards of their peers; the present moment mattered, not the next again year. New stylish clothes as worn by the trend-setters were promptly copied. The weekend dances and musical performances were very well attended. One estimate in 1959 calculated the teenagers spent 20% of their money on clothes, cosmetics and shoes; 17% on drink and cigarettes; 15% on sweets, snacks and soft drinks; the rest, almost half, went to many forms of pop entertainment, from cinemas and dance halls to sports, magazines and records. Spending was a device that gave a person identity and status, and most important, a sense of belonging to the group.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (May 2018)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Moral_panics" class="mw-redirect" title="Moral panics">Moral panics</a> break out in time of dramatic social change; they appeared often in the last two centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>  Teenager troubles first came to public attention during the war years, when there was a surge of juvenile delinquency.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> By the 1950s, there was widespread concern about bellicose American comic books that the boys were gobbling up; censorship was imposed in 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:CITESHORT" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:CITESHORT"><span title="More information is required to link this short citation to its long citation. (May 2018)">incomplete short citation</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2018)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> By that point, the media presented the teenagers in terms of generational rebellion. The  <a href="/wiki/Teddy_Boys" class="mw-redirect" title="Teddy Boys">Teddy Boys</a> were gangs that seemed prone to violence, in addition to their outlandish costumes.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>  Likewise, the 1960s working-class subculture known as "<a href="/wiki/Skinheads" class="mw-redirect" title="Skinheads">skinheads</a>" appeared ominous.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> The exaggerated moral panic among politicians and the older generation was typically belied by the growth in intergenerational co-operation between parents and children. Many working-class parents, enjoying newfound economic prosperity, eagerly took the opportunity to encourage their teens to enjoy more adventurous lives.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup>  Schools were falsely portrayed as dangerous blackboard jungles under the control of rowdy kids.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> The media distortions of the teens as too affluent, and as promiscuous, delinquent, counter-cultural rebels do not reflect the actual experiences of ordinary young adults, particularly young women,<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Starting in the late-1960s, the <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Counterculture movement">counterculture movement</a> spread from the United States like a wildfire.<sup id="cite_ref-Nelson1989_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson1989-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Bill Osgerby argues that:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>the counterculture\'s various strands developed from earlier artistic and political movements. On both sides of the Atlantic the 1950s "Beat Generation" had fused existentialist philosophy with jazz, poetry, literature, Eastern mysticism and drugs \xe2\x80\x93 themes that were all sustained in the 1960s counterculture.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAddisonJones2008132_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAddisonJones2008132-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>The UK did not experience the intense social turmoil produced in the US by the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> and racial tensions. Nevertheless, British youth readily identified with their American counterparts\' desire to cast off the older generation\'s social mores. Music was a powerful force. British groups and stars such as <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stones" class="mw-redirect" title="Rolling Stones">Rolling Stones</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">The Who</a>, <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">Led Zeppelin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">Pink Floyd</a> and many others gained huge followings in the UK and around the world, leading young people to question convention in everything from clothing to the class system.<sup id="cite_ref-Stark2009_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stark2009-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Faulk2016_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Faulk2016-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The anti-war movement in Britain was fueled by the counterculture. It collaborated with American counterparts, moving from an emphasis on nuclear war with Russia, to support for insurgents in the Southeast Asian jungles.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Educational_reform">Educational reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Educational reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_education_in_England" title="History of education in England">History of education in England</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Education_Act_1944" title="Education Act 1944">Education Act 1944</a> was an answer to surging social and educational demands created by the war and the widespread demands for social reform that approached utopianism.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> It was prepared by <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> MP <a href="/wiki/Rab_Butler" title="Rab Butler">Rab Butler</a> after wide consultation. The Act took effect in 1947 and created the modern split between <a href="/wiki/Primary_education" title="Primary education">primary education</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_education" title="Secondary education">secondary education</a> at the age of eleven years. The newly-elected Labour government adopted the <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Tripartite System">Tripartite System</a>, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Grammar_school" title="Grammar school">grammar schools</a>, <a href="/wiki/Secondary_modern_school" title="Secondary modern school">secondary modern schools</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_technical_school" title="Secondary technical school">secondary technical schools</a>, rejecting the comprehensive school proposals favoured by many in the Labour Party as more equalitarian.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>   Under the tripartite model, students who passed an exam were able to attend a prestigious grammar school. Those who did not pass the selection test attended <a href="/wiki/Secondary_modern_school" title="Secondary modern school">secondary modern schools</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Secondary_Technical_School" class="mw-redirect" title="Secondary Technical School">technical schools</a>. The school leaving age was raised to fifteen years. The <a href="/wiki/Public_school_(United_Kingdom)#1901\xe2\x80\x931959" title="Public school (United Kingdom)">elite system of public schools</a> was practically unchanged.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The new law was widely praised by the Conservatives because it honoured religion and social hierarchy, and by Labour because it opened new opportunities for the working-class, and also by the general public; because it ended the fees they previously had to pay.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> The Education Act became a permanent part of the <a href="/wiki/Post-war_consensus" title="Post-war consensus">Post-war consensus</a> supported by the three major political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>While the new law formed a part of the widely accepted <a href="/wiki/Post-war_consensus" title="Post-war consensus">Post-war consensus</a> agreed to in general by the major parties, one part generated <a href="/wiki/Debates_on_the_grammar_school" class="mw-redirect" title="Debates on the grammar school">controversy</a>. Left-wing critics attacked grammar schools as being elitist because a student had to pass a test at the age of eleven in order to enroll. Opponents, mostly in the Conservative Party, argued that grammar schools allowed pupils to obtain a good education through merit rather than through family income. By 1964, one in ten students were in <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_school" title="Comprehensive school">comprehensive schools</a> that did not sort children at the age of eleven. Labour education minister <a href="/wiki/Anthony_Crosland" title="Anthony Crosland">Anthony Crosland</a> (from 1965) crusaded to speed up the process.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> When Margaret Thatcher was appointed as Minister for Education in <a href="/wiki/1970_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1970 UK general election">1970</a>, one in three schools were comprehensives; The proportion doubled by 1974, despite her efforts to resist the trend against grammar schools. By 1979, over 90% of schools in the UK were comprehensives.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Higher_education">Higher education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Higher education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Universities_in_the_United_Kingdom#Expansion_after_1945" title="Universities in the United Kingdom">Universities in the United Kingdom \xc2\xa7&#160;Expansion after 1945</a></div>\n<p>Higher education expanded dramatically. Provincial university colleges were upgraded at Nottingham, Southampton and Exeter. By 1957, 21 universities were in existence. Expansion came even faster in the 1960s, with new universities such as: Keele, East Anglia, Essex, Kent, Sussex and York \xe2\x80\x94 bringing the total to 46 in 1970. Specialisation allowed national centres of excellence to emerge in Medicine at Edinburgh, engineering at Manchester, Science at Imperial College London, and Agriculture at Reading. Oxford and Cambridge; however, remained intellectually, culturally and politically dominant. They attracted top students from across the Commonwealth, but lost many of their best researchers to the United States, where salaries and research facilities were much more generous. Into the 1960s, student bodies remained largely middle and upper-class in origins; the average enrollment was only 2,600 in 1962. Every subsequent decade brought further expansion in numbers of institutions and students.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011357-64_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011357-64-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Tuition fees were introduced by Labour in 1998, raised to \xc2\xa33,000 a year in 2006, and passed \xc2\xa39,000 a year by 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> The tendency toward vocationalism and the decline in the humanities remained controversial, as well as a growing mindset among senior administrators that is pre-occupied with marketing (rankings) and accountability.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For the <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> the central postwar mission was to block threats from American private broadcasting and to continue <a href="/wiki/John_Reith,_1st_Baron_Reith" title="John Reith, 1st Baron Reith">John Reith\'s mission</a> of cultural uplift.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> The BBC remained a powerful force, despite the arrival of <a href="/wiki/ITV_(TV_network)" title="ITV (TV network)">Independent Television</a> in 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Newspaper barons had less political power after 1945. <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Koss" title="Stephen Koss">Stephen Koss</a> explains that the decline was caused by structural shifts: the major Fleet Street papers became properties of large, diversified capital empires with more interest in profits than politics. The provincial press virtually collapsed, with only the <i><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Guardian" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchester Guardian">Manchester Guardian</a></i> playing a national role; in 1964 it relocated to London. Growing competition arose from non-political journalism and from other media such as the BBC; independent press lords emerged who were independent of the political parties.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sport">Sport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Sport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Sport in the United Kingdom">Sport in the United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_England" title="Sport in England">Sport in England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Scotland" title="Sport in Scotland">Sport in Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Wales" title="Sport in Wales">Sport in Wales</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Sport in Northern Ireland">Sport in Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<p>Spectator sports became increasingly fashionable in postwar Britain, as attendance soared across the board.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> Despite the omnipresent austerity, the government were very proud to host the <a href="/wiki/1948_Summer_Olympics" title="1948 Summer Olympics">1948 Olympics</a>, even though Britain\'s athletes won only three gold medals compared to 38 for the Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> Budgets were tight and no new facilities were built. Athletes were given the same bonus rations as dockers and miners, 5,467 calories a day instead of the normal 2,600. Athletes were housed in existing accommodation. Male competitors stayed at nearby RAF and Army camps, while the women were housed in London dormitories.<sup id="cite_ref-Hampton2012_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hampton2012-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> Sporting competitions were minimal during the war years, but by 1948, 40 million a year were watching football matches, 300,000 per week went to motorcycle speedways and half a million watched greyhound races. Cinemas were jammed and dance halls were filled. The great cricket hero <a href="/wiki/Denis_Compton" title="Denis Compton">Denis Compton</a> was ultimately dominant; the <i>Daily Telegraph</i> reported he:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>made his run gaily and with a smile. His happy demeanour and his good looks completed a picture of the beau ideal of a sportsman. I doubt if any game at any period has thrown up anyone to match his popular appeal in the England of 1947-1949.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHennessy2006306,_316_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHennessy2006306,_316-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Football">Football</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Football">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Football_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Football in the United Kingdom">Football in the United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Football_in_England" title="Football in England">Football in England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Football_in_Scotland" title="Football in Scotland">Football in Scotland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Football_in_Wales" title="Football in Wales">Football in Wales</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Association_football_in_Northern_Ireland" title="Association football in Northern Ireland">Association football in Northern Ireland</a></div>\n<p>The most popular sport in the UK became association football; <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_F.C." title="Sheffield F.C.">Sheffield F.C.</a>, founded in 1857, is the world\'s oldest football club still in existence to the present day. The home nations all have separate national teams and domestic competitions, most notably England\'s <a href="/wiki/Premier_League" title="Premier League">Premier League</a> and <a href="/wiki/FA_Cup" title="FA Cup">FA Cup</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Premiership" title="Scottish Premiership">Scottish Premiership</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Cup" title="Scottish Cup">Scottish Cup</a>. Referred to as the "home of football" by the international governing body <a href="/wiki/FIFA" title="FIFA">FIFA</a>, England hosted and won the <a href="/wiki/1966_FIFA_World_Cup" title="1966 FIFA World Cup">1966 FIFA World Cup</a>. The English Premier League (formed in 1992 by member clubs of the old <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">Football League First Division</a>) is the most-watched football league in the world. Its biggest clubs include: <a href="/wiki/Manchester_United" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchester United">Manchester United</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_F.C." title="Liverpool F.C.">Liverpool</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arsenal_F.C." title="Arsenal F.C.">Arsenal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_F.C." title="Chelsea F.C.">Chelsea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manchester_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchester City">Manchester City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tottenham_Hotspur" class="mw-redirect" title="Tottenham Hotspur">Tottenham Hotspur</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Davies2011_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davies2011-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Scottish teams Celtic and Rangers also have a wide global fanbase.<sup id="cite_ref-Taylor2013_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2013-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Football in the UK is renowned for the intense rivalries between clubs and the passions of their supporters, which includes a tradition of <a href="/wiki/Football_chants" class="mw-redirect" title="Football chants">football chants</a>, such as, "<a href="/wiki/You%27re_Not_Singing_Any_More" title="You&#39;re Not Singing Any More">You\'re Not Singing Any More</a>" (or its variant "We Can See You Sneaking Out!"), sung by jubilant fans towards the opposition fans who have gone silent (or left early). Fans lined up for meat pies, burgers and chips. Football repealed its ban on floodlights in 1950, and night matches attracted increasingly large crowds of fans \xe2\x80\x93 some of them unruly; as well as large television audiences. Architects were challenged to build ever-larger stadia, as "their cantilevered constructions dwarfing mean streets, supplanted the cathedral as symbol of the city\'s identity and aspirations, and the fixtures surpassed church festivals in their national impact."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011386_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2011386-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="The_English_Disease:_football_hooliganism">The English Disease: football hooliganism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The English Disease: football hooliganism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Football_hooliganism" title="Football hooliganism">Football hooliganism</a></div>\n<p>Hooliganism at football matches, sometimes known as "the English disease", had been a characteristic for decades, but by the 1970s, confrontations had escalated into violence against visiting fans; including violence at matches held in mainland Europe. An in-depth study made in 1983 by High Court judge Sir <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Popplewell" title="Oliver Popplewell">Oliver Popplewell</a> found that many hooligans held steady jobs; they dressed in the latest style, and planned their violence ahead of time as a recreation in itself, to which football was a mere background. They were not poor and were searching for an identity more lively than their mundane working environment. By the 1990s, hooliganism had been sharply reduced, thanks to the use of seating-only stadiums where each spectator had to remain seated in an assigned seat, better policing methods, and the use of closed-circuit television to identify troublemakers.<sup id="cite_ref-Stewart2013_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart2013-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_sports">Other sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Other sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Cricket is England\'s other historic sport, but it grew faster in popularity in the overseas colonies, and immigrants in increasing numbers comprised the ranks of top players and fan base.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Tennis spread from upper-class estates into tennis clubs in middle-class suburbs, where it became a woman\'s specialty.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Women increasingly frequented gyms, which sprang up everywhere; by the mid-1990s, one in six members were women.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Middle-class men and women were usually more active than working-class people were. Scotland, the birthplace of golf, remains the top destination for the sport; many clubs opened up by 1910 and continue to operate to the present day. The number of golfers doubled in the 1950s from 500,000 to 1,000,000; mostly men. The total reached the 2.5 million mark by 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAddisonJones2008113_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAddisonJones2008113-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cinema">Cinema</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Cinema">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Cinema of the United Kingdom">Cinema of the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>The United Kingdom has had a significant film industry for over a century.<sup id="cite_ref-CurranPorter1983_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CurranPorter1983-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> While film output peaked in 1936, the "golden age" of British cinema occurred in the 1940s, during which the directors <a href="/wiki/David_Lean" title="David Lean">David Lean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Powell" title="Michael Powell">Michael Powell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Emeric_Pressburger" title="Emeric Pressburger">Emeric Pressburger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carol_Reed" title="Carol Reed">Carol Reed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Attenborough" title="Richard Attenborough">Richard Attenborough</a> produced their most highly acclaimed work. Many postwar British actors achieved international fame and critical success, including: <a href="/wiki/Maggie_Smith" title="Maggie Smith">Maggie Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Caine" title="Michael Caine">Michael Caine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sean_Connery" title="Sean Connery">Sean Connery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sellers" title="Peter Sellers">Peter Sellers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ben_Kingsley" title="Ben Kingsley">Ben Kingsley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Babington2001_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Babington2001-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> A handful of the films with the largest-ever box office returns have been made in the United Kingdom, including the second and third <a href="/wiki/List_of_highest-grossing_films#Highest-grossing_film_series" title="List of highest-grossing films">highest-grossing film series</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_(film_series)" title="Harry Potter (film series)">Harry Potter</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/James_Bond_(film_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="James Bond (film series)"><i>James Bond</i></a>).<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The identity of the British industry, and its relationship with the much larger and richer <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_the_United_States" title="Cinema of the United States">cinema of the United States</a>, has been the subject of debate. Film production in Britain has often been affected by attempts to compete with the American industry. The career of the producer <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Korda" title="Alexander Korda">Alexander Korda</a> was marked by this objective, the <a href="/wiki/Rank_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Rank Organisation">Rank Organisation</a> attempted to compete in the 1940s, and <a href="/wiki/Goldcrest_Films" title="Goldcrest Films">Goldcrest</a> in the 1980s. Numerous British-born directors, including <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Hitchcock" title="Alfred Hitchcock">Alfred Hitchcock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ridley_Scott" title="Ridley Scott">Ridley Scott</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> and performers, such as <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Chaplin" title="Charlie Chaplin">Charlie Chaplin</a><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cary_Grant" title="Cary Grant">Cary Grant</a>, achieved success primarily through their work in Hollywood.\n</p><p>In 2009, British films grossed around $2,000,000,000 worldwide and achieved a market share of around 7% globally and 17% in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-statistics_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-statistics-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup> UK box-office takings totalled \xc2\xa31,100,000,000 in 2012,<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> with 172.5&#160;million admissions.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immigration">Immigration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Immigration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Modern_immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="Modern immigration to the United Kingdom">Modern immigration to the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>After decades of low immigration, new arrivals became a significant factor after 1945. Immigration before 2000 was greatest from the former <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> especially <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>. Since 2000, large numbers have arrived from Europe and the Middle East.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The UK population was recorded as 56,267,000 people in 1996, out of which 52,942,000 were White. The other 3,307,000 represented diverse ethnic or racial origins: 875,000 were Black; 1,639,000 were Indian, Pakistani or Bangladeshi; 126,000 were Chinese; 161,000 were Other Asian; 506,000 were from other groups or were of mixed-race origin.<sup id="cite_ref-Royle1997_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Royle1997-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Some immigrants came to the UK as <a href="/wiki/Asylum_seekers" class="mw-redirect" title="Asylum seekers">asylum seekers</a>, seeking protection as <a href="/wiki/Refugees" class="mw-redirect" title="Refugees">refugees</a> under the United Nations <a href="/wiki/Convention_Relating_to_the_Status_of_Refugees" title="Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees">1951 Refugee Convention</a>, or from <a href="/wiki/Member_states_of_the_European_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Member states of the European Union">member states of the European Union</a>, exercising one of the European Union\'s <a href="/wiki/Four_Freedoms_(European_Union)" class="mw-redirect" title="Four Freedoms (European Union)">Four Freedoms</a>. Since the 1980s, however, the UK has become a leading proponent of European restrictionalism and has developed policies that tend to exclude asylum seekers from mainstream society. Dispersal policy was set up through the National Asylum Support Service programme so that asylum seekers were directed to urban areas that had available housing, although possibly because of a weak job market. While newly arrived asylum seekers and refugees had both skills and qualifications, they experienced high levels of unemployment, or else found mostly low-skilled jobs with low pay. Public opinion in host areas turned against them.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hynes2011_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hynes2011-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The new immigrants generally entered tight-knit ethnic communities. For example, the new Irish arrivals became integrated within a working-class <a href="/wiki/Irish_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Irish Catholic">Irish Catholic</a> environment that shaped their behaviour whilst maintaining a distinct ethnic identity in terms of religion, culture and Labour politics.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Spouses form the largest single category of migrant settlement, especially Indian, Pakistani and Bangladeshi women. For them it is a matter of restoring domestic relations in a familiar family environment, with higher incomes, thus easing the strain on the immigration process.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Fertility rates among new immigrants were much higher than the British norm (reflecting the norms of the country of origin). However, rates dropped rapidly with the length of stay in Britain. For some ethnic groups, fertility reached about the level of the UK national average (e.g., Black Caribbean) or below it (e.g., Indian and Chinese). Only among Pakistani and Bangladeshi women have total fertility rates remained well above the national average, but even so they have shown a continuous decrease since the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Enoch_Powell" title="Enoch Powell">Enoch Powell</a>, a Conservative MP, broke from the broad consensus supporting immigration in April 1968 to warn of long-term violence, unrest and internal discord should immigration continued from non-White countries. His <a href="/wiki/Rivers_of_Blood_speech" title="Rivers of Blood speech">speech foresaw "Rivers of Blood"</a> and predicted that White "native" English citizens would be unable to access social services and be overwhelmed by foreign cultures. While political, social and cultural elites were harshly critical of Powell and he was removed from the <a href="/wiki/Official_Opposition_Shadow_Cabinet" class="mw-redirect" title="Official Opposition Shadow Cabinet">Shadow Cabinet</a>, Powell developed substantial public support.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Thatcher.27s_Britain"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Thatcher\'s_Britain">Thatcher\'s Britain</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Thatcher&#039;s Britain">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Margaret_Thatcher" title="Premiership of Margaret Thatcher">Premiership of Margaret Thatcher</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thatcher_ministry_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Thatcher ministry (disambiguation)">Thatcher ministry</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Premier_Thatcher_tijdens_een_persconferentie,_Bestanddeelnr_932-7044.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Premier_Thatcher_tijdens_een_persconferentie%2C_Bestanddeelnr_932-7044.jpg/220px-Premier_Thatcher_tijdens_een_persconferentie%2C_Bestanddeelnr_932-7044.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Premier_Thatcher_tijdens_een_persconferentie%2C_Bestanddeelnr_932-7044.jpg/330px-Premier_Thatcher_tijdens_een_persconferentie%2C_Bestanddeelnr_932-7044.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Premier_Thatcher_tijdens_een_persconferentie%2C_Bestanddeelnr_932-7044.jpg/440px-Premier_Thatcher_tijdens_een_persconferentie%2C_Bestanddeelnr_932-7044.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2447" data-file-height="3674" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Premier_Thatcher_tijdens_een_persconferentie,_Bestanddeelnr_932-7044.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> (pictured in 1983) was Prime Minister of the United Kingdom from 1979-1990</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> was the dominant political force of the late-twentieth century, often compared to Churchill and <a href="/wiki/David_Lloyd_George" title="David Lloyd George">David Lloyd George</a> for her transformative agenda commonly referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a>". She was <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Leader of the Conservative Party (UK)">Leader of the Conservative Party</a> from 1975-1990, and Prime Minister from 1979-1990. She was often called the "Iron Lady" for her uncompromising politics and leadership style.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore2013_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore2013-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Political analyst <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Kavanagh" title="Dennis Kavanagh">Dennis Kavanagh</a> concludes that the "Thatcher Government produced such a large number of far-reaching changes across much of the policy spectrum, that it passes \'reasonable\' criteria of effectiveness, radicalism, and innovation."<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Labour Party under <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a> (Prime Minister 1976\xe2\x80\x9379) contested the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1979" title="United Kingdom general election, 1979">1979 general election</a> as unemployment passed the 1,000,000 mark and trade unions became more aggressive. The Conservatives used a highly effective poster created by advertisers <a href="/wiki/Saatchi_and_Saatchi" class="mw-redirect" title="Saatchi and Saatchi">Saatchi and Saatchi</a>, showing an unemployment queue snaking into the distance, carrying the caption "Labour isn\'t working". The Conservatives received 43.9% of the vote and 339 seats to Labour\'s 269, for an overall majority of 43 seats at the 1979 general election. Labour was weakened by the steady long-term decline in the proportion of manual workers in the electorate. Twice as many manual workers normally voted Labour as voted Conservative, but they now constituted only 56% of the electorate. When the Labour Party led by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a> narrowly won the 1964 general election, manual workers had accounted for 63%. Furthermore, they were beginning to turn against the trade unions \xe2\x80\x93 alienated, perhaps, by the difficulties of the winter of 1978\xe2\x80\x939. In contrast, Conservative policies stressed wider home ownership, which Labour refused to match. Thatcher did best in districts where the economy was relatively strong and was weaker where it was contracting.<sup id="cite_ref-ButlerKavanagh1997_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ButlerKavanagh1997-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Thatcherism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a></div>\n<p>As Prime Minister, she implemented policies focused on <a href="/wiki/Economic_liberalism" title="Economic liberalism">economic liberalism</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populism</a>, and pragmatism, known as <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Campbell2012_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell2012-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup>  Thatcher introduced a series of political and economic initiatives intended to reverse high unemployment and Britain\'s struggles in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_Discontent" title="Winter of Discontent">Winter of Discontent</a> and an ongoing recession. Her political philosophy and economic policies emphasised <a href="/wiki/Deregulation" title="Deregulation">deregulation</a> (particularly of the financial sector), flexible labour markets, the privatisation of <a href="/wiki/Government-owned_corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Government-owned corporation">state-owned companies</a> and reducing the power and influence of trade unions. Due to recession and high unemployment, Thatcher\'s popularity during her first years in office waned until the beginning of 1982, a few months before the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>. The afterglow of her victory at that war produced a resounding victory at the polls. She was <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1983" title="United Kingdom general election, 1983">re-elected in 1983 with an increased majority</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Privatisation was an enduring legacy of Thatcherism; it was accepted by the future Labour ministry of <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a>. Her policy was to privatise nationalised corporations (such as telephone and aerospace firms). She sold public housing to tenants, all on favourable terms. The policy developed an important electoral dimension during the second Thatcher government (1983\xe2\x80\x9387). It involved more than denationalisation: wider share ownership was the second plank of the policy.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Thatcher advocated an "enterprise society" in Britain, especially in widespread share-ownership, personal ownership of council houses, marginalisation of trade unions and expansion of private healthcare. These policies transformed many aspects of British society.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Thatcher was re-elected for a third term in <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987</a>. Although Labour made gains under the new leadership of <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>, compared to their landslide defeat at the previous election. During this period, her support for a <a href="/wiki/Poll_tax_(Great_Britain)" title="Poll tax (Great Britain)">Community Charge</a> (popularly referred to as "poll tax") was widely unpopular (especially in Scotland where the tax was enforced one year earlier than the rest of the country) and her negative views on the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Community</a> were not shared by others in her Cabinet. She lost support from Conservative MPs and resigned as Prime Minister of the United Kingdom and Leader of the Conservative Party in November 1990.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_movements">Cultural movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Cultural movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Environmentalism as a major public issue was brought to the forefront by Thatcher in 1988, when she included a manifesto warning about climate change.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a> movements of the 1980s reduced the emphasis on <a href="/wiki/Intensive_farming" title="Intensive farming">intensive farming</a>, and promoted <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation</a> of the countryside.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Protestant religious observance declined notably in Britain during the second half of the twentieth century. Catholicism (based on the Irish elements) held its own, while <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Islam in the United Kingdom">Islam</a> grew rapidly due to immigration from Asia and the Middle East as well as higher birth rates from that sector of the general population. <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> attendance particularly dropped, although <a href="/wiki/Charisma" title="Charisma">charismatic</a> churches like <a href="/wiki/Elim_Pentecostal_Church" title="Elim Pentecostal Church">Elim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assemblies_of_God_in_Great_Britain" title="Assemblies of God in Great Britain">AOG</a> grew. The movement to <a href="/wiki/Keep_Sunday_Special" title="Keep Sunday Special">Keep Sunday Special</a> seemed to have lost at the beginning of the twenty-first century.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_change">Economic change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Economic change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Household_prosperity">Household prosperity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Household prosperity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>From 1964-1996, income per head doubled, while ownership of various household goods significantly increased. By 1996, two-thirds of households owned cars, 82% had central heating, most people owned a VCR, and one in five houses had a home computer.<sup id="cite_ref-Radice1996_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radice1996-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> In 1971, 9% of households had no access to a shower or bathroom, compared with only 1% in 1990; largely due to demolition or modernisation of older properties that lacked such facilities. In 1971, only 35% had central heating, while 78% enjoyed this amenity in 1990. By 1990, 93% of households had colour television, 87% had telephones, 86% had washing machines, 80% had deep-freezers, 60% had video-recorders and 47% had microwave ovens. Holiday entitlements became more generous. In 1990, nine out of ten full-time manual workers were entitled to more than four weeks of paid holiday a year, while twenty years previously; only two-thirds had been allowed three weeks or more.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The post-war period also witnessed significant improvements in housing conditions. In 1960, 14% of British households had no indoor toilet, while in 1967; 22% of all homes had no basic hot water supply. By the 1990s, however almost all homes had these amenities together with central heating, which had been a luxury just two decades earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Troubles_of_1970s_and_after">Troubles of 1970s and after</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Troubles of 1970s and after">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Deindustrialisation">Deindustrialisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Deindustrialisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<p>After 1960, British industries were troubled, a phenomenon sometimes known as the "<a href="/wiki/The_British_disease" title="The British disease">British Disease</a>". The railways were decrepit, more textile mills closed than opened, steel employment fell sharply and the automotive industry practically disappeared, apart from some luxury models. <a href="/wiki/Deindustrialisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Deindustrialisation">Deindustrialisation</a> meant the closure of many operations in mining, heavy industry and manufacturing, with the resulting loss of high paid working-class jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> A certain amount of turnover had always taken place, with newer businesses replacing older ones. However, the 1970s were different, with a worldwide energy crisis and a dramatic influx of low-cost manufactured goods from Asia leading to more closures and fewer openings. Major sectors were hit hard between 1966-1982, with a 60% decline in textiles, 53% in metal manufacture, 43% in mining, 38% in construction, and 35% in vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison2009295_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison2009295-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> Coal mining quickly collapsed and practically disappeared in the 21st century. The consumption of coal, mostly for electricity\xe2\x80\x94plunged from 157,000,000 tonnes in 1970 to 37,000,000 tonnes in 2015, nearly all of it imported. Coal mining jobs fell from a peak of 1,191,000 in 1920 to 695,000 in 1956, 247,000 in 1976, 44,000 in 1993 to 2,000 in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup> In the 1970s, manufacturing accounted for 25% of the economy. Total employment in manufacturing fell from 7.1 million in 1979 to 4.5 million in 1992 and only 2.7 million in 2016, when it accounted for 10% of the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECookStevenson2014167-68_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECookStevenson2014167-68-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Scotland, deindustrialisation took place rapidly in the 1970s and 1980s, as most of the traditional industries drastically shrank or completely closed. A new service-oriented economy emerged to replace them.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Finlay2004_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Finlay2004-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> In 1954, Scottish shipyards built 12% of the world\'s tonnage, falling to 1% in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeight2013403_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeight2013403-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/North_Sea_oil" title="North Sea oil">North Sea oil</a> created a major new industry after 1970, and some older firms successfully took advantage of the opportunity. <a href="/wiki/John_Brown_%26_Company" title="John Brown &amp; Company">John Brown &amp; Company</a>\'s shipyard at Clydebank transformed itself from a traditional shipbuilding business to a factor in the high technology offshore oil and gas drilling industry.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Popular response varied.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> Some nostalgically invoked a glorious industrial past or the bygone British Empire to cope with their newfound personal economic insecurity. Others looked to the EU for help. Some turned to exclusionary Englishness as the solution to current grievances.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> By the 21st century, enough grievances had accumulated to have a political impact. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Independence_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom Independence Party">United Kingdom Independence Party</a> (UKIP), based in White working-class towns, increased its share of the vote while warning against the dangers of continued, unrestricted immigration from the European Union. The political reverberations came to a head in the shocking and unexpected vote in favour of <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">the UK leaving the EU</a> at the <a href="/wiki/2016_EU_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="2016 EU referendum">EU referendum</a> on 23 June 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deregulation">Deregulation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Deregulation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Thatcher\'s deregulation of the economy ended the post-war consensus about the planned economy. She was elected during a period of crises between the Labour Party and the trade unions, and an increasing trend of higher unemployment and deindustrialisation. She also <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_(financial_markets)" title="Big Bang (financial markets)">liberalised the City of London</a> and privatised state-owned enterprises. Inflation fell and trade union influence was significantly reduced.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Union_of_Mineworkers_(Great_Britain)" title="National Union of Mineworkers (Great Britain)">National Union of Mineworkers</a> (NUM) had for a long  time, been one of the strongest trade unions. Its strikes had toppled the Heath ministry at the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_February_1974" title="United Kingdom general election, February 1974">February 1974 general election</a>. Thatcher drew the line and defeated it at the bitterly fought <a href="/wiki/UK_miners%27_strike_(1984%E2%80%9385)" title="UK miners&#39; strike (1984\xe2\x80\x9385)">miners\' strike of 1984\xe2\x80\x931985</a>. The basic problem was that the easy coal had all been mined and what was left was very expensive and uneconomical. The miners, however, were fighting not just for higher wages; but for a way of life that had to continue had to be subsidised by other workers. The Union split. In the end, almost all the coal mines were shut down. Britain turned to its vast reserves of <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> gas and oil, which brought in substantial tax and export revenues, to fuel a new economic boom.\n</p><p>After the economic boom of the 1980s, a brief but severe recession occurred between 1990-92, mostly under the ministry of <a href="/wiki/John_Major" title="John Major">John Major</a>; who succeeded Thatcher as Prime Minister and Conservative Party leader in November 1990. The pound was ejected from the <a href="/wiki/Exchange_Rate_Mechanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange Rate Mechanism">Exchange Rate Mechanism</a> on <a href="/wiki/Black_Wednesday" title="Black Wednesday">Black Wednesday</a> in September 1992, an event which was humiliating for the Conservative government but which helped boost the recovery. The rest of the 1990s saw a period of continuous economic growth that lasted over sixteen years and was greatly expanded under Blair\'s <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a> government following his landslide election victory at 1997 general election, with a rejuvenated Labour Party abandoning its commitment to old policies like nuclear disarmament and nationalisation of key industries, and no reversal of the Thatcher-led union reforms. Many traditional Labour supporters were unhappy with Blair abandoning <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialism" title="Democratic socialism">socialism</a> and the restructuring of Clause IV in 1995; effectively tearing up the constitution which had put socialist values and common ownership of industry at heart of party policy for nearly eighty years. Blair promoted the Labour Party as "<a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a>", a social democratic centrist party for the 21st century which promised to inject new life into Britain; with investment in education made a key priority.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Britain\'s wish to join the Common Market (as the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> was known in Britain) was first expressed in July 1961 by the Macmillan ministry, was negotiated by <a href="/wiki/Edward_Heath" title="Edward Heath">Edward Heath</a> as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Privy_Seal" title="Lord Privy Seal">Lord Privy Seal</a>, but was vetoed in 1963 by <a href="/wiki/President_of_France" title="President of France">French President</a> <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>. After initially hesitating over the issue, Wilson lodged a second application (in May 1967) to join the <a href="/wiki/European_Community" class="mw-redirect" title="European Community">European Community</a>, as it was then called. Like the first, though, it was again vetoed by de Gaulle.<sup id="cite_ref-A_History_Of_The_British_Labour_Party_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_History_Of_The_British_Labour_Party-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Conservative Prime Minister Heath negotiated terms for admission and the UK finally joined the Community on 1 January 1973. The Labour Party, who were deeply divided over the issue (though Wilson remained in favour) returned to government in 1974, although two general elections had to be held in the UK that year. Following a renegotiation of Britain\'s terms of entry by Foreign Secretary <a href="/wiki/James_Callaghan" title="James Callaghan">James Callaghan</a>, a <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Communities_membership_referendum,_1975" title="United Kingdom European Communities membership referendum, 1975">referendum</a> was duly held on 5 June 1975, on which Cabinet ministers were permitted to campaign on opposite sides, an exception to the normal rule of <a href="/wiki/Cabinet_collective_responsibility" title="Cabinet collective responsibility">Cabinet collective responsibility</a>. The proposition to continue membership was passed with a substantial majority.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Devolution_for_Scotland_and_Wales">Devolution for Scotland and Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Devolution for Scotland and Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish devolution">Scottish devolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/Welsh_devolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh devolution">Welsh devolution</a></div>\n<p>On 11 September 1997 (the seven-hundredth anniversary of the Scottish victory over the English at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stirling_Bridge" title="Battle of Stirling Bridge">Battle of Stirling Bridge</a>) a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_devolution_referendum,_1997" title="Scottish devolution referendum, 1997">referendum</a> was held on devolving substantial power to a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Parliament" title="Scottish Parliament">Scottish Parliament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> Voters overwhelmingly voted to establish a Scottish parliament and grant it limited taxation powers. Two weeks later, a <a href="/wiki/Welsh_devolution_referendum,_1997" title="Welsh devolution referendum, 1997">referendum</a> on establishing a <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Assembly" class="mw-redirect" title="Welsh Assembly">Welsh Assembly</a> was also approved, by a narrow majority. The first elections were held, and these bodies began to operate, in 1999. The creation of these bodies widened the differences between regions, especially in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Healthcare in the United Kingdom">healthcare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Since_1997">Since 1997</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Since 1997">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tony_Blair_and_New_Labour">Tony Blair and New Labour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Tony Blair and New Labour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/New_Labour" title="New Labour">New Labour</a>, <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Tony_Blair" title="Premiership of Tony Blair">Premiership of Tony Blair</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Blair_ministry" title="Blair ministry">Blair ministry</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> became the Leader of the Labour Party in 1994, and served as Prime Minister from 1997\xe2\x80\x932007. With <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>, he founded the movement known as New Labour. In domestic policy, Blair sought to modernise Britain\'s public services, encourage enterprise and innovation in its private sector and keep the economy open to international commerce. The Northern Irish, Scottish and Welsh devolutions took place under his premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-Seldon2007_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seldon2007-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Kavanagh argues that by the 1980s, left-wing or socialist tendencies in the Labour Party divided the party and united its enemies:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Labour voters are not attracted by many "socialist" policies, that is greater public ownership, comprehensive education, extending trade union rights, and redistribution. Such policies appear to unite supporters of other parties in rejection well serving to divide Labour voters.<sup id="cite_ref-Kavanagh1990_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kavanagh1990-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Blair moved the Labour Party in new directions, minimising the left-wing or socialist factions. He thereby broadened the appeal to  professionals and middle-class voters in "<a href="/wiki/Middle_England" title="Middle England">Middle England</a>", who had traditionally voted Conservative.\n</p><p>Blair was also anxious to escape from the Labour Party\'s reputation for "tax-and-spend" domestic policies; he wanted instead to establish a reputation for fiscal prudence. He had undertaken in general terms to modernise the welfare state, but he had avoided undertaking to reduce poverty, achieve full employment, or reverse the increase in inequality that had occurred during the Thatcher years. Once in office, however, his government launched a package of social policies designed to reduce unemployment and poverty. The commitment to modernise the welfare state was tackled by the introduction of "welfare to work" programmes<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> to motivate the unemployed to return to work instead of drawing benefit. Poverty reduction programmes were targeted at specific groups, including children and the elderly, and took the form of what were termed "New Deals".<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> There were also new Tax Credit allowances for low-income and single-parent families with children, and "Sure Start" progammes for under-fours in deprived areas. A "National Strategy for Neighbourhood Renewal"<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> was launched in 2001 with the objective of ensuring that "within 10 to 20 years no-one should be seriously disadvantaged by where they live"; a "Social Exclusion Unit"<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources.
