(May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> was set up, and annual progress reports concerning the reduction of poverty and social exclusion were commissioned.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a>  <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a> replaced Blair as <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_Gordon_Brown" title="Premiership of Gordon Brown">Prime Minister in 2007</a>. Labour\'s popularity declined further with the onset of a <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">worldwide recession in 2008</a>, where the Conservatives led by David Cameron overtook Labour in the polls for the first time in many years. In Scotland, the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">SNP</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Lib Dems</a> managed to win seats from Labour at by-elections in a further blow to the government. Arguably, the controversial decision for the UK to support the <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_Iraq" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of Iraq">invasion of Iraq in 2003</a> sparked the beginning of Labour\'s decline in popularity; as their majority was significantly reduced at the <a href="/wiki/2005_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="2005 UK general election">2005 general election</a>. Five years later, Labour lost 91 seats in the House of Commons at the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a>, the party\'s biggest loss of seats at a single general election since <a href="/wiki/1931_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1931 UK general election">1931</a>. On 11 May 2010, Brown was succeeded as Prime Minister by <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> of the Conservative Party, and resigned as Leader of the Labour Party on the same day after nearly three years. <a href="/wiki/Ed_Miliband" title="Ed Miliband">Ed Miliband</a> was elected as the new Labour leader on 25 September. Miliband being elected leader is seen as marking the end of the "New Labour" era.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservatives_return">Conservatives return</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Conservatives return">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Premiership_of_David_Cameron" title="Premiership of David Cameron">Premiership of David Cameron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cameron_ministry_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Cameron ministry (disambiguation)">Cameron ministry</a></div>\n<p>The economic damage done by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Recession" title="Great Recession">Great Recession</a> weakened Labour\'s image and facilitated a Conservative comeback after thirteen years in opposition. Since his election as Conservative Party leader in 2005, <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> (2010-2016) sought to rebrand the Conservatives, embracing an increasingly socially liberal position; as opposed to the socially conservative values the party traditionally advocated. The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2010" title="United Kingdom general election, 2010">2010 general election</a> resulted in a hung parliament, the first in 36 years; and led to Cameron becoming Prime Minister as the head of a <a href="/wiki/Cameron%E2%80%93Clegg_coalition" title="Cameron\xe2\x80\x93Clegg coalition">coalition government</a> with the centrist <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>. His premiership was marked by the ongoing negative economic effects of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x9308">late-2000s worldwide financial crisis</a>. He faced a large deficit in government finances that he sought to reduce through austerity measures. His government introduced large-scale changes to <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Welfare state in the United Kingdom">welfare</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_immigration_law" title="United Kingdom immigration law">immigration policy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Education_in_England" title="Education in England">education</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Healthcare_in_England" title="Healthcare in England">healthcare</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> His government privatised the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a> and some other state assets, and legalised <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriage</a> in July 2013. He was <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2015" title="United Kingdom general election, 2015">re-elected in 2015</a>, with 330 seats in the House of Commons; enabling him to form a majority government. This result was unexpected, as another hung parliament was predicted by most major polls. Cameron formed the first Conservative majority government since <a href="/wiki/1992_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1992 UK general election">1992</a>, while Labour lost nearly all its Scottish seats to the SNP in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/2014_Scottish_independence_referendum" class="mw-redirect" title="2014 Scottish independence referendum">Scottish independence referendum</a> and Miliband resigned as party leader.\n</p><p>On the morning of Friday 24 June 2016, when the results of the EU referendum were announced, Cameron announced his intention to step down as Prime Minister and Leader of Conservative Party at the Conservative Party conference in the autumn of that year. Following the British electorate\'s <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">vote to Leave the European Union in a nationwide referendum</a>; with his government having campaigned for a "Remain" vote. He resigned earlier than intended on 13 July 2016, and was succeeded by former Home Secretary <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>, who called <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_2017" title="United Kingdom general election, 2017">another general election</a> for 8 June 2017, resulting in a hung parliament. The Labour Party, now under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>, made a net gain of seats for the first time in 20 years, and 30 new seats were gained by Labour overall; 6 of which were in Scotland. Notably, <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Canterbury (UK Parliament constituency)">Canterbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kensington_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Kensington (UK Parliament constituency)">Kensington</a> had never returned Labour MPs to Parliament before, but both were narrowly gained at the expense of the Conservative Party. Most significantly was the 9.6% swing from Conservative to Labour, which was the largest swing from one party to another since <a href="/wiki/1945_UK_general_election" class="mw-redirect" title="1945 UK general election">1945</a>. As for the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrats</a>, former party leader and Deputy Prime Minister Nick Clegg lost his <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Hallam_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Sheffield Hallam (UK Parliament constituency)">Sheffield Hallam</a> seat to Labour, and former Secretary of State for Business Sir Vince Cable regained the Twickenham seat from the Conservatives two years previously. Cable succeeded Tim Farron as Lib Dem leader after the election, and three new seats were  gained for the party in Scotland. As predicted, UKIP led by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Nuttall" title="Paul Nuttall">Paul Nuttall</a> made no gains and lost the majority of its previous supporters to the Labour and Conservative parties; signifying an end to multi-party politics and a return to two-party politics. Nuttall stood at <a href="/wiki/Boston_and_Skegness_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Boston and Skegness (UK Parliament constituency)">Boston and Skegness</a>, which was the constituency with the highest vote to leave the EU at the referendum. However, Nuttall finished in third place and resigned as UKIP leader.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_growth_and_crises_since_1995">Economic growth and crises since 1995</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Economic growth and crises since 1995">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing_2">Housing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Housing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_housing_affordability_take_home_pay.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/UK_housing_affordability_take_home_pay.png/220px-UK_housing_affordability_take_home_pay.png" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/UK_housing_affordability_take_home_pay.png/330px-UK_housing_affordability_take_home_pay.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/UK_housing_affordability_take_home_pay.png/440px-UK_housing_affordability_take_home_pay.png 2x" data-file-width="3825" data-file-height="2100" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UK_housing_affordability_take_home_pay.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>United Kingdom housing affordability as described by mortgage payments as a percentage of take home pay from 1983 to 2015</div></div></div>\n<p>House prices tripled in the 20 years between 1995 and 2015. Growth was almost continuous during the period, save for a two-year period of decline around 2008 as a result of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007\xe2\x80\x9308">banking crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>\nThe gap between income and house prices has changed in the last 20 years such that even in the most affordable regions of England and Wales buyers have to spend six times their income. It was most marked in London, where in 2013 the \xc2\xa3300,000, <a href="/wiki/Median" title="Median">median</a> house price costs 12 times the median London income of \xc2\xa324,600.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brexit">Brexit</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Brexit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Brexit" title="Brexit">Brexit</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aftermath_of_the_United_Kingdom_European_Union_membership_referendum,_2016" title="Aftermath of the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016">Aftermath of the United Kingdom European Union membership referendum, 2016</a></div>\n<p>Economic and social issues caused political unrest, particularly in areas hurt by deindustrialization and globalization of the economy. The <a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UK Independence Party</a> (UKIP), a <a href="/wiki/Euroscepticism" title="Euroscepticism">Eurosceptic</a> political party, 1993. It rose to prominence after 2000, winning third place in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2004_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2004 (United Kingdom)">2004 European elections</a>, second place in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2009 (United Kingdom)">2009 European elections</a> and first place in the <a href="/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2014_(United_Kingdom)" title="European Parliament election, 2014 (United Kingdom)">2014 European elections</a>, with 27.5% of the total vote.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> Cameron won reelection in 2015 in part by promising a referendum on the EU, which he expected would easily defeat Brexit.\n</p><p>The \'Leave\' pro-Brexit campaign waxed strong primarily on the need to control sovereignty and migration,  whereas the \'Remain\' campaign focused on the negative economic impacts of leaving the EU.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Polls showed more cited both the EU (32%) and migration (48%) as important issues than cited the economy (27%).<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup>  By 2018 as the complexities of leaving the EU dominated political discussions, economists produced gloomy projections of the damage to the British economy.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war_consensus">Post-war consensus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Post-war consensus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post-war_consensus" title="Post-war consensus">Post-war consensus</a></div>\n<p>The <i>post-war consensus</i> is a historians\' model of political agreement from 1945 to 1979, when newly elected Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Thatcher" title="Margaret Thatcher">Margaret Thatcher</a> rejected and reversed it. .<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The concept claims there was a widespread consensus that covered support for a coherent package of policies that were developed in the 1930s, promised during the Second World War, and enacted under Attlee. The policies dealt with a mixed economy, Keynesianism, and a broad welfare state.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> In recent years the validity of the interpretation has been debated by historians.\n</p><p>The historians\' model of the post-war consensus was most fully developed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Addison" title="Paul Addison">Paul Addison</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Addison2011_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Addison2011-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> The basic argument is that in the 1930s <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal Party</a> intellectuals led by <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Beveridge" title="William Beveridge">William Beveridge</a> developed a series of plans that became especially attractive as the wartime government promised a much better post-war Britain and saw the need to engage every sector of society. The coalition government during the war, headed by Churchill and Attlee, signed off on a series of white papers that promised Britain a much improved welfare state after the war. The promises included the national health service, and expansion of education, housing, and a number of welfare programmes. It did not include the nationalisation of all industries, which was a Labour Party design. The Labour Party did not challenge the system of elite public schools\xe2\x80\x94they became part of the consensus, as did comprehensive schools. Nor did Labour challenge the primacy of Oxford and Cambridge. However, the consensus did call for building many new universities to dramatically broaden educational base of society. Conservatives did not challenge the socialised medicine of the National Health Service; indeed, they boasted they could do better job of running it.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> In foreign policy, the consensus called for an anti-Communist <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> policy, decolonisation, close ties to NATO, the United States, and the Commonwealth, and slowly emerging ties to the European Community.<sup id="cite_ref-Gordon1969_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gordon1969-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The model states that from 1945 until the arrival of Thatcher in 1979, there was a broad multi-partisan national consensus on social and economic policy, especially regarding the welfare state, nationalised health services, educational reform, a mixed economy, government regulation, Keynesian macroeconomic policies, and full employment. Apart from the question of nationalisation of some industries, these policies were broadly accepted by the three major parties, as well as by industry, the financial community and the labour movement. Until the 1980s, historians generally agreed on the existence and importance of the consensus. Some historians such as <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Miliband" title="Ralph Miliband">Ralph Miliband</a> expressed disappointment that the consensus was a modest or even conservative package that blocked a fully socialised society.<sup id="cite_ref-Miliband1972_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Miliband1972-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Angus_Calder" title="Angus Calder">Angus Calder</a> complained bitterly that the post-war reforms were an inadequate reward for the wartime sacrifices, and a cynical betrayal of the people\'s hope for a more just post-war society.<sup id="cite_ref-Calder2012_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Calder2012-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> In recent years, there has been a historiographical debate on whether such a consensus ever existed.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_history_of_the_United_Kingdom_(1945%E2%80%93present)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_England#Since_1945" class="mw-redirect" title="Social history of England">Social history of England \xc2\xa7&#160;Since 1945</a></li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Spirit_of_%2745" title="The Spirit of &#39;45">The Spirit of \'45</a></i>, a 2013 documentary film</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
